10.1016/j.jmp.2020.102476
Episodic memory entails encoding, storing, and later retrieving information about experienced events. Memory encodings have explicit sensory features, but they also have internally generated propositions and activated associations that are generated by the individual ( Chechile, 2018 ). But from the science of physics, we also know that it is necessary to describe the physical world in terms of both space and time. It would thus seem reasonable to suppose that time and order should be also encoded into the memory representation of the world. Yet the recognition and understanding of the significance of time and order for memory emerged slowly in psychological science.

In the nineteenth century, clues about the potential import of time and order can be found from the study of forgetting. For example, a key forgetting principle articulated by  Jost (1897)  and  Ribot (1882)  stressed that there is a greater tendency for an interfering event to disrupt a recent memory trace rather than an older memory. It is difficult to see how this principle could be true without time playing a role in the encoding and the organization of information in memory. However the laws of Ribot and Jost can be accounted for by a consolidation theory of forgetting ( Müller & Pilzecker, 1900 ). A simple metaphor for consolidation is the idea that new memories are like a painting where the paint can be disrupted before it becomes hardened. So it was not necessary for researchers to posit a temporal organization for memory to account for the findings of Ribot and Jost.

Later in the twentieth century, a number of procedures in experimental psychology were employed that focused on temporal or ordinal information. These tasks include: (1) subjective judgment of elapsed time ( Hicks, Miller, & Kinsbourne, 1976 ), (2) subjective judgment of the relative duration between two remembered intervals ( Block, 1985 ), (3) subjective estimation of the lag between item repetitions ( Hinrichs & Buschke, 1968 ), (4) judgment of the position of recalled items ( Michon & Jackson, 1984 ), (5) dating of long-term world events ( Underwood, 1977 ), (6) selection of the more recent of two provided items ( Fozard, 1970 ), and (7) rearranging items provided in a random order to match the original order ( Shimamura, Janowsky, & Squire, 1990 ). 
                         1 
                       
                      
                         1 
                         The study of the learning of a serial list of items is a topic that has attracted considerable interest in experimental psychology. But serial list learning is not included as a method that targets specifically temporal or ordinal information. The well known U-shaped serial position curve can be accounted for by either the chaining of item-to-item associations, the learning of item-to-position associations, or a combination of those two mechanisms. Thus the serial position curve itself does not mandate the encoding of temporal information. Consequently serial learning is not included in the above list of seven procedures designed specifically to focus on either time or order. But some accounts of serial learning do use a hybrid model of temporal codes with association information (e.g.,  Caplan, 2015 ). 
                       The data from these tasks led some researchers to believe that temporal information was encoded in memory. For example,  Underwood (1977)  described a simple study where item knowledge for eight world events was excellent, but only two of 108 participants could correctly rank order all the events. Yet the overall rank order of the participants was correlated with the correct order.  Underwood (1977, 1983)  argued that an imprecise temporal code could be developed as a separate attribute of memory. That is, the learning of order was something additional to the learning of the items per se. But  Estes (1985)  argued that the strength model of  Hinrichs (1970)  could potentially account for data such as the judgments of the relative recency of events. In strength theory each item is presumed to have a strength that decays in time, and it is assumed that stronger memory traces are judged as being more recent. Although Estes preferred instead a perturbation model ( Estes, 1972, 1985; Lee, 1992; Lee & Estes, 1981 ), he nonetheless acknowledged that a strength model, which does not specifically encoding temporal or ordinal information, was a creditable account. 
                         2 
                       
                      
                         2 
                         In the perturbation model, items on a list are located in a position buffer. Without the rehearsal of the list, the items are perturbed from their initial location with the passage of time. 
                       It can be argued that none of the existing behavioral procedures in the twentieth century provided unimpeachable evidence that people were encoding temporal or ordinal information. Without physiological evidence for temporal encoding, psychologists could reasonably be skeptical that temporal information was being stored in memory. Moreover it was widely accepted that fact learning can encode clock time without time itself being linked to the information (e.g., a person might remember, by looking at their watch, that it was 10:15 a.m. when they received a telephone call from an old friend).  Friedman (1993)  advanced an extreme version of this idea and argued that time is solely constructed from the memories of facts. He claimed that the recollection of events occurs by a reconstructive process without having temporal features being biologically generated and stored. Although this hypothesis is possible, it is difficult to establish this view as superior to other theories that do rely on some coding of temporal or ordinal information being generated by the individual.

Further insights about time did emerge from theneurosciences. For example, a number of investigators have identified cells in the hippocampi that are sensitive to sequential and temporal information ( Eichenbaum, 2014; MacDonald, Lepage, Eden, & Eichenbaum, 2011; Naya & Suzuki, 2011; Pastalkova, Itskov, Amarasingham, & Buzsáki, 2008 ). 
                         3 
                       
                      
                         3 
                         A classic claim in physiological psychology was that no new cells are formed in the developed brain ( Ramón y Cajal, 1913 ). Yet subsequent research has found evidence of new cell growth — a process called  neurogenesis .  Aimone, Wiles, and Gage (2006)  advanced a neurogenesis model that resulted in the formation of clusters that are organized temporally.  Cai et al. (2016)  provided experimental evidence of hippocampal-cell ensembles that behave as anticipated from the model of  Aimone et al. (2006) . 
                       Other animals studies provide support for an important role of the frontal and temporal lobes for ordinal processing ( Kesner, Hopkins, & Fineman, 1994; Mangels, 1997; Milner, Corsi, & Leonard, 1991; Shimamura et al., 1990 ). Using event-related fMRI scans on humans, evidence was provided that both the temporal lobe and the prefrontal cortex are active on tasks that require the processing of temporal information ( Jenkins & Ranganath, 2010 ). In addition, brain rhythms were identified that were active when animals were engaged in a learning task (e.g.,  O’Keefe & Recce, 1993 ).  Wearden (1994, 2001)  showed that a coarse biological clock can be achieved by the collective coding of a series of oscillators that have different frequencies, so the various brain rhythms are a possible basis for achieving a temporal coding. In fact, this general idea was endorsed in the OSCAR computational model for serial order ( Brown, Preece, & Hulme, 2000 ).

At a theoretical level in the twenty-first century, some researchers have argued that episodic memory is roughly organized along a temporal dimension (e.g.,  Brown, Neath, & Chater, 2007; Brown et al., 2000; Chechile, 2018; Howard & Eichenbaum, 2015; Howard et al., 2014; Howard, Shankar, Aue, & Criss, 2015; Ward, Gallistel, & Balsam, 2013 ). But  Howard and Eichenbaum (2015)  also stressed that the temporal flow of memory can have jumps and breaks because the recall of an item can trigger a prior time, which in turn can trigger other content information. Moreover, it is possible to scan memory by using time as the search framework.

Although the temporal coding of information is a reasonable hypothesis, it is not yet established that time must be a key feature of memory organization and storage. We have not yet reached a consensus model in psychology about how people and animals solve the processing problem of developing a mental model of events that roughly reflects the order in which events actually unfold in time. Some investigators (e.g.,  Friedman, 1993 ) had argued that the experimental data can be accounted for by hypotheses that do not involve the positing of a temporal code. But despite the lack of a clear theoretical consensus, progress toward understanding the memory of order can be achieved by the development of improved psychometric methods. The existing dependent variables for studying the order of events are limited because they do not account for guessing factors and do not account for several stages or degrees of order knowledge. There are many other examples in cognitive psychology where the behavioral dependent variables involve an entangled combination of latent processes, but with model-based measurement these latent processes were estimated. See  Chechile (2018)  for a more general review of how multinomial processing tree (MPT) models can be used to augment the toolkit of memory researchers by extracting underlying processes from the available behavioral measures. MPT models also have desirable statistical properties that provide for a distribution-free analysis of between-condition differences. Importantly, even for a single experimental condition, MPT models provide for an informative measure of the separate latent processes. The current paper is focused on the development of such a memory measurement tool. The measurement procedure is called the Event Order (EO) model. The EO model is used, along with other modeling tools, to provide evidence that order knowledge is a separate memory attribute or feature of the memory representation from the other stimulus aspects of the event.

Unlike other general theories in cognitive psychology, MPT models are not generic models across a wide range of experimental tasks. Intentionally MPT models are designed for measuring a set of basic underlying cognitive processes for a specific experimental protocol.  Chechile (2018)  described a number of novel experimental procedures that were developed to obtain the necessary information for a specific MPT model. The EO model is similarly designed for a specific experimental procedure that is described in the next subsection.

Although time and order are clearly interlinked, it is important to observe that these attributes are potentially different. If absolute (or clock) time were encoded, then the decision about the order between two events is trivially determined. But if temporal information is not stored with the events, then whatever is stored in memory has to be used to answer questions about order. So it is difficult to claim that subjective estimates of time are inherently valid. Researchers who use subjective time estimates implicitly presume as factually true that time is encoded and is directly accessible. But it is difficult to be certain that the time judgments are valid, especially when they do not correspond to the actual duration. Consequently the focus in this paper is on assessing the memory for order rather than time. By studying order we are not presuming the format of the information encoded in memory. Thus of the seven procedures identified in the introduction, only the last two are pertinent for the assessment of order knowledge. That is, method (6), the selection of the more recent of two presented items and method (7), the rearrangement of a random list of the items to match the presented order, deal with order knowledge. But both of these procedures have important limitations. Method 6 has too high of a guessing rate and not enough granularity to be an effective procedure for getting at the underlying order knowledge; whereas method 7 is too laborious for participants except for very short lists. Also if the participants have partial order knowledge, then they may give up trying to arrange the entire list. Consequently neither of the existing methods are a suitable basis for providing the data needed for an MPT model of the underlying processes associated with ordering events. But a modification of method 6, which involves the testing of a triplet of events, expands the order possibilities, and it provides considerable information about the participants knowledge of the three items, especially if the testing protocols have follow up inquiries of the participants.

The EO method first requires that the participants experience a series of events (e.g., a list of stimuli presented one at a time). At the test phase, the participants are given a set of probed tests about a triplet of items. Let us denote the three targets for a triplet, which is being probed, as

The process tree for the EO model is displayed in  Fig. 2 . The model is in terms of four states for the participant’s knowledge of the order of the three probed target items. These states are: (1) complete knowledge of all three ordinal relationships among the triplet of stimuli, (2) knowledge of two of the three ordinal relationships, (3) knowledge of only a single order relationship, and (4) the null state of no order knowledge whatsoever. For any experimental condition, there are probabilities for the population proportions for these four states. Both cases exemplify the state of complete order knowledge of the triplet. We are not concerned about differentiating between these two mental representations. Effectively these two mental representations are behaviorally equivalent.

While it is certain that a cell 1 response occurs if the participants has knowledge of all three orders, but a cell 1 response can also occur as a consequence of the order knowledge being in any of the other three states. The EO model is essentially an elaborate method for correcting for guessing. From the processing tree, the probabilities for the four response cells can be expressed in terms of the probabilities for the four knowledge states. Notice that only a response in category 4 is unambiguous. A cell 4 response would not occur if there were any order knowledge of the events. The ambiguity in the other three response categories is typical of an entanglement problem in cognitive psychology where multiple cognitive states or conditions might result in the same behavioral response. This feature is characteristic of behavioral measures. A case has been made that the dependent measures in cognitive psychology potentially involve multiple underlying processes, so cognitive dependent variables are  generally not process pure measures of a single cognitive state or process  ( Chechile, 2007, 2018; Chechile & Roder, 1998 ).  Chechile (2018)  reviewed a number of approaches for measuring the underlying cognitive processes and argued that model-based measurement is needed to uncover the latent information. An MPT model can probabilistically unpack the latent structure provided that: (1) the overall data are sufficiently rich, (2) the model is valid, and (3) the model is identifiable. The MPT model formally examines and encodes the entanglement for a special task that is designed for measuring the desired cognitive information. The multiple pathways to a response category are represented by the processing tree. Given the data for all the trials for an experimental condition, it is possible to estimate the probabilities for the four knowledge states. It is assumed that the data for a condition are independent and identically distributed. The statistical estimation of the proportions in the four knowledge states is a probabilistic separation of the entangled cognitive states. For example, for a single participant on a single trial, who gives a cell 2 response, it is not known if that single outcome was in the 2c state, the 1c state, or in the null state N as shown in the process tree. We only learn about the partition among the three tree pathways for a cell-2 response probabilistically. Thus MPT models are designed to solve an entanglement measurement problem by means of the statistical analysis of the relevant aggregate data for a condition. 
                            5 
                          
                         
                            5 
                            If the actual order was  
                                  
                                     A 
                                     B 
                                     C 
                                  
                                and the participant selects instead  
                                  
                                     C 
                                     B 
                                     A 
                                  
                                on the 6-AFC test, then it is reasonable to conclude that the person has no order knowledge for this single triplet. Although that is a reasonable conclusion, we still require the same experimental protocols of testing the person again on a 5-AFC task. We cannot assume in advance which of the other three response cells to score this triplet of events without actually implementing the testing protocols. The frequencies in all four categories are needed to execute the probabilistic partitioning across the four knowledge states. The goal is not to draw conclusions based on a single test trial. 
                         
                         
                            6 
                          
                         
                            6 
                            An MPT can be applied to a single individual in a single experimental condition by examining many trials for that same person on the basic task for that condition. The key feature for the statistical separation is the repetition of the task to obtain frequencies for the multinomial categories.

Although a MPT model might seem compelling and straightforward, it is nonetheless important to formally establish that the model is identifiable. This issue is separate from the statistical estimation of parameters because parameters can be estimated even for some unidentified models ( Chechile, 1977 ). But before proving that the EO model is identifiable, it is useful to first discuss some notation and some important definitions.

The EO model, which is specified in Eqs.  (1) – (4) , uses two different sets of population parameters. One set of parameters denotes the population proportions for the multinomial categories (i.e., the set of parameters

Identifiability for a MPT model is also dependent on a key property of the set of mapping functions  
                            F 
                         
 ( Chechile, 1998, 2004 ). The following definition will help clarify the demonstration of model identification.

Definition 3 
                            Unique Identifiable (UI) Mapping. Consider a mapping function between a domain vector space  
                                  X 
                                to a range vector space  
                                  Y 
                                as a set of vector pairs where the first vector of the pair is in the domain space and the second vector of the pair is in the range space. A UI mapping is a set of vector pairs such that no two of the pairs has the same domain vector and no two of the pairs has the same range vector.

Unique Identifiable (UI) Mapping. Consider a mapping function between a domain vector space  
                                  X 
                                to a range vector space  
                                  Y 
                                as a set of vector pairs where the first vector of the pair is in the domain space and the second vector of the pair is in the range space. A UI mapping is a set of vector pairs such that no two of the pairs has the same domain vector and no two of the pairs has the same range vector.

Note that the term  UI  is different from the term  one-to-one  that is commonly used by other writers. One-to-one mappings also take uniquely different points in the domain space to uniquely different points in the range space, but one-to-one mappings have the additional property of being  onto . Onto mappings cover all the points in the range space (i.e., there are no points in the range space that have not been mapped). Hence one-to-one functions are more restrictive mappings than a UI mapping. A UI mapping is equivalent to what some writers call an  injective non-surjective function  ( James & James, 1992 ).

Chechile (1998)  established the necessary and sufficient condition for a multinomial model to be likelihood identifiable. That is

Theorem 1 
                            
                               Model  
                               
                                  
                                     M 
                                     
                                        ( 
                                        
                                        Θ 
                                        , 
                                        
                                        Φ 
                                        , 
                                        
                                        F 
                                        
                                        ) 
                                     
                                  
                                
                               for a multinomial or a set of independent multinomials is likelihood identified if and only if the mapping  
                               
                                  F 
                                
                               from  
                               
                                  θ 
                               
                               -space to  
                               
                                  ϕ 
                               
                               -space is a UI mapping.

Model  
                               
                                  
                                     M 
                                     
                                        ( 
                                        
                                        Θ 
                                        , 
                                        
                                        Φ 
                                        , 
                                        
                                        F 
                                        
                                        ) 
                                     
                                  
                                
                               for a multinomial or a set of independent multinomials is likelihood identified if and only if the mapping  
                               
                                  F 
                                
                               from  
                               
                                  θ 
                               
                               -space to  
                               
                                  ϕ 
                               
                               -space is a UI mapping.

The proof of the theorem can be found in  Chechile (1998) , so it is omitted here. In essence the proof consists of first establishing the fact that no two points exist in the entire  
                            ϕ 
                         -space representation of a set of independent multinomials that have the same likelihood function. Consequently the subset of  
                            ϕ 
                         -space that the  
                            F 
                          function maps to from  
                            θ 
                         -space also does not have any two points with the same likelihood function. Also because  
                            F 
                          is a UI mapping, none of the mapped vectors are many-to-one. Consequently all points in  
                            θ 
                         -space have different likelihood functions. Thus the model is identifiable (i.e., there are no observational equivalent points in  
                            θ 
                         -space).

The following theorem applies the above theorem and definitions to the EO model.

Theorem 2 
                            
                               The EO model is likelihood identified.

The EO model is likelihood identified.

Statistical estimation of the EO model parameters can be obtained by using several different techniques. In this section two methods for parameter estimation are described in detail. The first approach uses the population parameter mapping (PPM) method, and the second approach employs the maximum likelihood estimation (MLE) procedure. The PPM method is a Bayesian Monte Carlo sampling procedure that is not based on a Markov chain Monte Carlo (MCMC) algorithm. Because PPM method is less familiar to many investigators, there is a comparison example in the last subsection between the PPM estimation and a different Bayesian solution via a MCMC implementation of the EO model.

The PPM method was developed and discussed in a series of papers ( Chechile, 1998, 2004, 2010a, 2010b, 2020; Chechile, Sloboda, & Chamberland, 2012; Chechile & Soraci, 1999 ). This method is a two-step Bayesian Monte Carlo sampling procedure for generating random vectors of  
                            θ 
                         -space points from the posterior distribution. The first step is to draw a random vector from the posterior distribution from the  
                            ϕ 
                         -space variates. The Bayesian analysis for any multinomial in terms of the response categories (i.e., the  
                            ϕ 
                         -space) is well known. Both the prior and the posterior distribution (in terms of the  
                            ϕ 
                         -space variates associated with the categories of the multinomial data) are Dirichlet distributions. The Dirichlet distribution is a generalization of the beta distribution ( Lindley, 1964; Mauldon, 1959 ). See  Chechile (2020)  for an entire chapter dealing with the mathematical properties of the Dirichlet distribution. The joint posterior density function of the posterior Dirichlet distribution for a multinomial with

The Dirichlet is a natural Bayesian conjugate function, (i.e., if the prior is a Dirichlet distribution, then the posterior distribution must also be a member in the Dirichlet family). The values of the shape parameters specify a particular Dirichlet distribution from the Dirichlet family.                             Fisher, a major architect and defender of orthodox statistical inference, argued against the flat prior distribution because it was not invariant under nonlinear transformation of the parameter ( Fisher, 1922 ). However  Jeffreys (1946)  provided a prior distribution that was invariant under nonlinear transformation. The Jeffreys prior for multinomial data is a Dirichlet distribution where  
                                  
                                     
                                        
                                           α 
                                        
                                        
                                           i 
                                        
                                        
                                           ′ 
                                        
                                     
                                     =

                                     = 
                                     1 
                                     , 
                                     
                                     … 
                                     , 
                                     k 
                                     + 
                                     1 
                                  
                               . It is important to note that the flat prior and Jeffreys prior are trivially different when the observed frequencies are sizable. That is, the data swamp the differences between the two low-information prior distributions.  Chechile (2020)  provided an illustration of this point for a case where the sample size was as small as 12.  Chechile (2020)  also showed that the flat prior had less Shannon information than the Jeffreys prior.

                            k

                            8 
                          
                         
                            8 
                            Researchers, who are very experienced with Markov Chain Monte Carlo (MCMC) methods, should not confuse this Monte Carlo sampling from the Dirichlet distribution as a MCMC application. The sampling procedure described in  Theorem 3  is not based on a Markov chain nor is it based on ergodicity theory. It does not require a burn-in phase, and it does not depend on an initial starting guess. Moreover all the random samples are independent, unlike the autocorrelation issue that can occur with MCMC samples. Consequently all the samples drawn from the Dirichlet distribution are independent and valid samples from the joint posterior distribution.

                            θ 
                         -space points from the posterior distribution. These R based vectors are then used for all subsequent point and interval estimates as well as for between-condition statistical tests.

There are 14 special cases where one or more of the multinomial cell frequencies equals zero. For these cases the MLE differs from that shown in Eq.  (16) . The MLEs for these special cases are provided in the  Appendix .

The parameters of the EO model can also be estimated by a MCMC Bayesian analysis. Unlike the PPM method, the prior distribution and posterior distribution is in terms of the  
                            θ 
                          parameters of the MPT model rather than in terms of the  
                            ϕ 
                          parameters of the multinomial and mapped to the  
                            θ 
                          parameters. A particular straightforward MCMC estimation method for the EO model is to employ the  Metropolis-within-Gibbs  algorithm ( Chechile, 2020; Geweke & Tunizaki, 2001 ). The MCMC procedure is a slower algorithm, and is based on the assumptions of ergodicity theory, so it converges asymptotically to the posterior distribution, whereas the PPM method avoids those limitations. In fact the chief reason for the PPM method originally was to circumvent problems with MCMC sampling methods for the case of MPT models. Nonetheless the MCMC procedure can estimate the parameters of the EO model. To illustrate this point, let us consider a case where the frequencies

Prior to using a MPT model, it is advantageous to learn about its statistical properties because those studies help to provide useful information about the method of parameter estimation, the choice of sample size, and the method of averaging estimates over a group of participants. In this section two series of Monte Carlo studies are explored. The first series is devoted to assessing the relative accuracy of the point estimates for the PPM and MLE methods as a function of sample size. The second series is focused on the issue of pooling versus averaging of the estimates.

MLE and PPM estimates for a MPT model parameters can differ( Chechile, 2004, 2010a, 2010b; Chechile et al., 2012 ). These studies provided evidence from Monte Carlo sampling experiments that the PPM estimates were more accurate than the corresponding MLE values for a number of specific MPT models. In the Monte Carlo studies, data were randomly sampled from known or stipulated population values for the underlying MPT parameters. Based on these frequencies, both MLE and PPM estimates were found. The mean absolute-value error over all the Monte Carlo simulations was found for both estimation methods. Although the mean absolute-value error decreased for both estimation methods as a function of increasing sample size, the PPM errors were uniformly smaller. Given these results, it is also important to ascertain the relative accuracy of the PPM and MLE methods for the EO model.

To assess the error of the MLE and PPM estimation methods as a function of sample size, 1000 Monte Carlo samples were generated for configurations of the four parameters of the EO model, where the sum of the parameters for a configuration was 1. For each sampled configuration, we know the true population values of the EO parameters. Based on those true values, a random sample of frequencies  
                            
                               
                                  n 
                               
                               
                                  i 
                               
                            
                         ,  
                            
                               i 
                               = 
                               1 
                               , 
                               … 
                               , 
                               4 
                            
                          was produced by means of the multinomial likelihood distribution. Based on the same set of frequency values that were sampled, the MLE and PPM estimates of the EO model parameters were computed. The mean absolute-value difference between the estimate and the true value was found for each parameter. The resulting mean errors are shown in  Table 1  as a function of sample size. Note as the sample size increases, there is a reduction in the mean estimation error, but the MLE values are never smaller than the corresponding PPM estimates. Thus the PPM method results in a more accurate estimate for the EO model. Others have also observed the fact that the maximum likelihood estimate is far from being an optimal estimator, especially in the practical world of finite sample sizes. For example, Le Cam observed that “In view of Fisher’s vast influence, it is perhaps not surprising that the presumed superiority of the method is still for many an article of faith promoted with religious fervor. This state of affairs remains, in spite of a long accumulation of evidence to the effect that maximum likelihood estimates are often useless, or grossly misleading” ( Le Cam, 1986 , p. 622).

The above Monte Carlo simulations also provide useful information about the  
                               
                                  P 
                                  
                                     ( 
                                     c 
                                     o 
                                     h 
                                     ) 
                                  
                               
                             statistic, which is associated with the PPM method. These results are shown in  Table 2 .

If there are no observations whatsoever (i.e., the case of a non-informative prior distribution), then the mean of the  
                               
                                  P 
                                  
                                     ( 
                                     c 
                                     o

                                     ) 
                                  
                               
                             statistic is

                                  P 
                                  
                                     ( 
                                     c

                                     ) 
                                  
                               
                             metric increases with sample size. Hence, on average for a sample size of 400, the posterior-to-prior odds ratio for  
                               
                                  P 
                                  
                                     ( 
                                     c 
                                     o 
                                     h 
                                     ) 
                                  
                               
                             is 8.14. Consequently for the PPM procedure, the PPM statistic of  
                               
                                  P 
                                  
                                     ( 
                                     c 
                                     o 
                                     h 
                                     ) 
                                  
                               
                             can help build strength for the model itself, despite the fact that the EO model has three free parameters for a four-category multinomial (i.e., it is a saturated model).

Many frequentist researchers, working with MPT models, seek to reduce the number of free parameters in order that a classical  
                               
                                  
                                     G 
                                  
                                  
                                     2 
                                  
                               
                             statistical test can be employed to statistically assess the model. However the beauty of the EO model is that it measures all four possible states of order knowledge about a triplet of events. Thus the model has high construct validation for measuring what it should be measuring. It would be a shame to make a potentially incorrect simplification of the EO model just to conduct a classical  
                               
                                  
                                     G 
                                  
                                  
                                     2 
                                  
                               
                             statistical test. Nonetheless, the data from any particular experiment might involve some participants who were willfully behaving contrary to their real order knowledge in an act of rebellion, or perhaps the participants simply misunderstood the experimental instructions. Thus the model might be incorrect for some special circumstances, so it is valuable to have a way to detect these aberrant cases. Actually, there is a way to detect model misspecification of the EO model with the PPM  
                               
                                  P 
                                  
                                     ( 
                                     c 
                                     o 
                                     h 
                                     ) 
                                  
                               
                             metric (see  Chechile, 2010a ). To illustrate the utility of  
                               
                                  P 
                                  
                                     ( 
                                     c 
                                     o 
                                     h 
                                     ) 
                                  
                               
                             for detecting model incoherence, consider any of the cases listed in  Table 3 . The relative ratio of the frequencies is the same for each case, but the sample size varies. Notice for each case that the value of  
                               
                                  P 
                                  
                                     ( 
                                     c 
                                     o 
                                     h 
                                     ) 
                                  
                               
                             has decreased compared to the prior value of .0833, which is the  
                               
                                  P 
                                  
                                     ( 
                                     c 
                                     o 
                                     h 
                                     ) 
                                  
                               
                             value before any data are collected. Thus the posterior-to-prior odds ratio has decreased rather than increased, thereby building a case against the EO model. That is, the prior-to-posterior odds ratio is evidence against the EO model for that experiment. As the sample size increases, the magnitude of the prior-to-posterior odds ratio increases for the artificial cases listed in  Table 3 , which makes it increasingly clear that the EO model estimates are not to be trusted for those artificial “data”.

The cases listed in  Table 3  also are problematic for the MLE method. Because the frequencies in each category are nonzero, the MLE values can be found by means of Eq.  (16) .                                   06 
                               
                            . This combination of parameter values maximizes the likelihood function, but with numbers that are not consistent with the probability interpretation of the parameters. A researcher might assume that the likelihood function needs to be limited to values on the three-dimensional simplex, which would be consistent with a probabilistic interpretation for the EO model parameters. But the frequentist researcher adopting this search strategy would actually be violating a basic premise of frequentist statistics by imposing prior knowledge as a part of the process for estimating the parameters. Imposing constraints on parameters is effectively adopting a prior distribution for the configuration of the model parameters, which is an operation that is not allowed within the frequentist framework. A proper frequentist MLE maximizes the likelihood function regardless of the spatial location of the parameters. For simple models, such as the models for the cell proportions  
                               
                                  
                                     ϕ 
                                  
                                  
                                     i 
                                  
                               
                            ,  
                               
                                  i 
                                  = 
                                  1 
                                  , 
                                  … 
                                  , 
                                  4 
                               
                            , the MLE finds the reasonable solution of  
                               
                                  
                                     
                                        
                                           
                                              ϕ 
                                           
                                           
                                              ˆ 
                                           
                                        
                                     
                                     
                                        i 
                                     
                                  
                                  = 
                                  
                                     
                                        
                                           
                                              n 
                                           
                                           
                                              i 
                                           
                                        
                                     
                                     
                                        n 
                                     
                                  
                               
                            . But in terms of the latent parameters, the MLE configuration can result in values outside the three-dimensional simplex. The first author of this paper has encountered this problem previously with the MLE method in the first MPT model that was developed for measuring storage and retrieval probabilities ( Chechile, 1973 ). For that very reason he adopted henceforth Bayesian methods to formally encode prior knowledge with a probability distribution on the model parameters. With the Bayesian PPM procedure only a small subset of the 50,000 mapping attempts are coherent, and these few random samples from the  
                               ϕ 
                             space are the basis of the model estimates for each case in  Table 3 . Consequently the PPM solution for the artificial data are within the three-dimensional simplex, but the corresponding  
                               
                                  P 
                                  
                                     ( 
                                     c 
                                     o 
                                     h 
                                     ) 
                                  
                               
                             statistic of the mapping process is a strong signal that there is a problem with applying the EO model for these hypothetical cases. 
                               9 
                             
                            
                               9 
                               
                                  Chechile (2004)  discussed cases where there are rival MPT models for the same data. For the PPM method, the same posterior Dirichlet distribution is sampled from, but the coherent mapped vectors of  
                                     θ 
                                   space parameters are nonetheless different for the rival models. The  
                                     
                                        P 
                                        
                                           ( 
                                           c 
                                           o 
                                           h 
                                           ) 
                                        
                                     
                                   parameter for the different models was a very valuable statistic for detecting the better of the two models.

Chechile (2009)  addressed a basic question about the statistical analysis of MPT models concerning the relative accuracy of two different methods for estimating the parameters for a given experimental condition. One approach (the averaging method) is based on estimating the parameters for each participant separately and then averaging those values. The other approach (the pooling method) first groups the frequencies for each multinomial category across all the participants and then estimates the MPT model parameters. In general these two methods do not yield the same results.  Chechile (2009, p. 568)  proved a general condition, which must be satisfied for the two methods to be identical, but that condition is rarely satisfied. Importantly, the estimates for the EO model parameters do not satisfy this equivalence condition. Consequently the two methods of analysis will yield different values, so the question of the relative accuracy of the pooling and averaging methods is important to assess for the EO model.

The results from a Monte Carlo study of the difference between the averaging and pooling methods are shown in  Table 4 . For these results the true population values for the

Thus these results are consistent with the findings obtained for four other MPT models that were previously examined (viz.,  Chechile, 2009 ).

The general superiority of the pooling method might strike some as a surprising finding. Yet from a Bayesian perspective this outcome is reasonable. One property of a Bayesian analysis is that the final posterior distribution is not dependent on the order by which the data are examined. Bayes theorem can, in principle, be used before and after each observation. That is, the posterior distribution after the first observation becomes the prior distribution for the second observation. The final posterior distribution after  
                            
                               
                                  n 
                               
                               
                                  r 
                               
                            
                          serially processed observations is equal to the distribution of a Bayesian analysis that is based on a single batch of  
                            
                               
                                  n 
                               
                               
                                  r 
                               
                            
                          observations. Similarly the pooling method, which is based on a single batch of ( 
                            
                               
                                  
                                     n 
                                  
                                  
                                     g 
                                  
                               
                               
                                  
                                     n 
                                  
                                  
                                     r 
                                  
                               
                            
                         ) observations, results in the same distribution to that of a serial set of Bayesian analyses that processed the data one participant at a time. The pooling method thus is based on a consistent set of priors. That is, the prior distribution before any participants are tested might be taken as a non-informative prior, but the prior distribution for each succeeding participant is different so as to encode what was learned from the previously tested participants. The averaging method ignores this accumulated information, and this neglect of information results in reduced accuracy.

Despite the demonstration that the averaging method is less accurate for the EO model as well as for the four models examined in  Chechile (2009) , some reviewers have nonetheless suggested that the averaging method might be better for cases where there are wide individual differences. While a complete examination of individual differences for MPT models is outside the scope of the current paper, a test case can be explored for the EO model. Although pooling is a more accurate estimation method for the EO model, there are nonetheless settings where the investigator needs to obtain the estimation on an individual-subject basis. For example, in a clinical application it is often important to assess the model measurement on an individual basis. For such applications,  Chechile (2010b)  recommended a jackknifed estimate for the individual. The jackknifed value is a weighted difference between two pooled estimates of the parameter. One estimate is based on pooling all the data except for patient  
                            i 
                         , whereas the other estimate is based on pooling all patients including patient  
                            i 
                         . 
                            10 
                          
                         
                            10 
                            The inaccuracy of an estimator based on the averaging of some metrics has been observed by others. For example  Quenouille (1956)  detected what he called bias, and he proposed a procedure to reduce the magnitude of the bias.  Tukey (1958)  later called this procedure the jackknife method. Consequently some frequentist statisticians were aware of the problems with averaging under some circumstances. Yet no formal theorems were introduced within the frequentist framework to account for why the jackknife method reduced the magnitude of estimation bias. It was an empirical finding that the jackknife method helps to reduce bias. One might speculate that perhaps the efficacy of the jackknife procedure is because it is based on two pooled estimates, which individually are more accurate. 
                         
                         Chechile (2010b)  provided a detailed discussion of a study that used the jackknifed MPT estimation method for a group of Korsakoff amnesic patients and for a yoked group of control patients. In principle this approach can also be used for the EO model if there is a similar requirement to obtain parameter estimates on an individual basis. 
                            11 
                          
                         
                            11 
                            
                               Estes (1956)  argued that pooling data can mask the detection of a correct model for the changes across experimental conditions. In essence a modeling error might result from the pooling process that is similar to the error that occurs with Simpson’s paradox. For such cases, the researcher still has the option of using the individual data, despite substantial estimation error, to see if the models across conditions are different from the models based on jackknifed estimates.

The EO method can be used to examine order knowledge as a function of several experimental factors. In the first two experiments there are three factors that are examined. One factor is the  spacing  between stimuli in the learning phase. The stimuli can be either  adjacent ,  near  (three apart), or  far  (15 apart). The second factor is the  stimuli relatedness  (i.e., either  unrelated  or  related ). The third factor is the number of  learning trials . In experiment 1 there were two learning trials; whereas in experiment 2 there were four learning trials.

In experiment 1, 60 students at Tufts University volunteered to help satisfy a participation requirement in their course (either Introductory Psychology or Introductory Statistics). In experiment 2, there was a separate group of 117 volunteers examined from the same participant pool.

The stimuli for each list were 40 words that were presented, one at a time, at the rate of 2 s per word in the learning phase of a trial. Among the words on a list, there were six clusters of three words that were semantically related (e.g., BOOK NEWSPAPER MAGAZINE or DIAMOND RUBY EMERALD). 
                               12 
                             
                            
                               12 
                               For experiment 1 the word categories were: precious stones, units of time, relatives, distance measures, metals, reading material, emotions, seasons, fruits, bodies of water, military ranks, pets, fabrics, utensils, religious buildings, parts of speech, furniture, body parts, political office, crimes, tools, medical professions, sports, chemical elements, musical instruments, coins, birds, transportation, dances, trees, fish, languages, directions, cards, and candy types. For experiment 2, half of these categories were used. 
                             For two of these six clusters, the words were in adjacent serial positions; two other clusters the words were separated by three intervening words, and two clusters were separated by 15 intervening words. The other words on the list did not come from a common semantic category. No words were repeated across different lists.

After the entire word list was presented, the participant received six tests for six separate triplets of words using the EO experimental protocol, which is illustrated in  Fig. 1 . For each test phase, 18 of the 40 words were probed in the six triplets. For the test phase on the next presentation for the same list, a different set of 18 words is probed. The first two words and the last two words on the list were not tested. Three of the tests were for triplets of related words, and three of the tests were for triplets with unrelated words. Among the three tests for either the related condition or the unrelated condition, there was one group of words in the  adjacent  condition, one group in the  near  separation condition, and one triplet of words that were in the  far  separation condition. Following the testing for all six triplets, the testing phase of the trial was completed. In experiment 1 each of six separate lists of words were presented twice. In experiment 2 each of three lists was presented four times where the words probed on the third and fourth repetitions were the same as the words probed for the respective first and second test trials. All participants had the same lists and had the same order of the words on the list.

The stimulus presentation and data collection were conducted by means of a Python program using the PsychoPy software package. The time for the experiment took between 40 to 50 min depending on the participant.

The frequencies for the multinomial categories for each condition for both experiments are listed in  Table 5 . There are no missing values for either experiment. The corresponding PPM estimates for the EO model parameters are shown in  Table 6 .

Following the recommendation in  Chechile (2020) , a Bayes factor greater than or equal to  
                            
                               19 
                            
                          (either  
                            
                               B 
                               
                                  
                                     F 
                                  
                                  
                                     10 
                                  
                               
                            
                          or  
                            
                               B 
                               
                                  
                                     F 
                                  
                                  
                                     01 
                                  
                               
                            
                         ) is sufficiently strong evidence to warrant reporting as a reliable effect (i.e., it is a “good bet – too good to disregard”). Moreover  Chechile (2020)  provided a utility-theory rationale for the optimal scientific decision being to not decide in favor of either  
                            
                               
                                  H 
                               
                               
                                  0 
                               
                            
                          or  
                            
                               
                                  H 
                               
                               
                                  1 
                               
                            
                          if the above Bayes factor criterion fails to be met. The utility theory analysis in  Chechile (2020)  is based on the utilities and disutilities of making correct versus incorrect decisions where a non-decision can be a credible option. From this framework, a statistical hypothesis test in experimental science is best done in a context where both the null and the alternative hypotheses are creditable (i.e., they are interval hypotheses rather than testing a point null hypothesis). 
                            13 
                          
                         
                            13 
                            
                               Chechile (2020)  also discussed the possible use of a Bayes factor for a point null hypothesis, but this application is recommended only for some rare cases when the scientist is attempting to build support for a true null. Because a point hypothesis has a probability mass of  
                                  0 
                               , it is scientifically foolish to attempt to build evidence against a hypothesis that already has a probability of  
                                  0 
                               . Consequently,  Chechile (2020)  argues that nearly all of the applications of point hypothesis testing in the life sciences are not suitable for attempting to build support for a true null. However, he did consider an application from the physical sciences that assessed predictions from the Standard Model in particle physics and where the Bayes factor for a point null hypothesis increased as the result of new experimental evidence. 
                          Moreover, from this framework it is recommended that researchers only make scientific claims for either the null or alternative hypothesis when the Bayes factor is sufficiently large. In this paper, all the hypotheses that are tested are interval hypotheses.

For testing the effect of learning trials, the  
                            
                               
                                  θ

A stacked bar chart is like a vertical pie chart. Inspection of this display clearly shows: (1) that there is an improvement in order knowledge after the second training trial, (2) that increased word relatedness improves order knowledge, (3) that the spacing is best for items that are adjacent and worse for items in the near separation condition, and (4) that order knowledge is still far from mastery after only two training trials.

Overall Results for Experiment 2

See  Fig. 4  to visualize all the conditions with a single set of stacked bar charts. Note the similarity of this figure to the stacked bar charts for experiment 1 shown in  Fig. 3 . The two experiments clearly establish that (1) the repetition of training improves order knowledge, (2) order knowledge is improved for related items, and (3) the spacing of the stimuli is best for adjacent items and worse for items in the near separation condition.

At this point one might reasonably raise the question about the degree to which insufficient order knowledge is simply a consequence of insufficient item storage. For example, suppose item A was not represented in memory, then it is unlikely that the participant would be successful in selecting the correct order for any triplet that included item A.

Questions about the role of insufficient item storage have implications for the premise that order is a separate and different attribute of memory. For example, suppose order information was just an inseparable component of item content, then the premise that order is a different attribute of memory from event knowledge can be reasonably challenged. But if item storage and order storage are separate components of memory, then order storage should not be perfectly predicted on the basis of only item storage. Consequently it is important to measure both order storage and item storage in the same experiment. In this section of the paper, a model for obtaining item storage is discussed.

A suitable item-storage model is based on a series of forced-choice recognition tests that is modified slightly from the experimental protocol described in  Chechile and Soraci (1999) . The Chechile–Soraci MPT model is discussed extensively in  Chechile (2020) . The Chechile–Soraci testing procedure involves an initial recall task that is followed by a series of forced-choice recognition tests. The modification of that procedure for the current research is to drop the initial recall test. 
                         14 
                       
                      
                         14 
                         With the inclusion of an initial recall test, it was possible in the Chechile–Soraci study to estimate storage, retrieval, and fractional storage. But because in the current research we are not interested in the measurement of retrieval, the recall test trials are omitted. This modification shortens the experimental running time and simplifies the item-storage model. 
                       The item-storage testing protocol uses an initial four-alternative forced-choice test where one of the items on the learning list is presented along with three other novel items that were not on the list (i.e., foils). If the participant is correct on the 4-AFC task, then that is a cell 1 response for a separate multinomial, which is shown in panel A of  Fig. 5 . But if the person is incorrect on the 4-AFC task, then the incorrectly selected foil item is removed from the set of alternatives, and the participant is instructed to try again on a 3-AFC test. If the participant is correct on the 3-AFC test, then that is a cell 2 response; otherwise the person must select again on a 2-AFC test. Note that the frequencies in the four-item multinomial illustrated in  Fig. 5  are  
                         
                            
                               
                                  n 
                               
                               
                                  1 
                                  
                                     ( 
                                     i 
                                     ) 
                                  
                               
                            
                            , 
                            
                            … 
                            
                            
                               
                                  n 
                               
                               
                                  4 
                                  
                                     ( 
                                     i 
                                     ) 
                                  
                               
                            
                         
                       in order to distinguish these frequencies from the order-testing multinomial frequencies. The process tree itself for this experimental task is shown in panel B of  Fig. 5 .

The item model assumes that an item is in one of three states for item knowledge. The three states correspond to (1) sufficient storage, (2) fractional or partial storage, and (3) the null state of no storage whatsoever; the probability for these respective states over the items are

                      . In addition, there are two cases for partially stored items. There is partial storage if the participant can reject either one or two of the three foil items on the 4-AFC test. If the persons cannot reject any of the foils, then that is essentially the case represented by non-storage. If the person can reject all three foils on the 4-AFC test, then that is essentially case represented by sufficient storage. Because there are two types of fractional storage, there is a parameter

The goal in experiment 3 is to study both item storage and order knowledge within the same experiment. With this type of data, we can assess whether or not order knowledge is an inseparable aspect of item learning. If there were statistical support for the alternative hypothesis, then that result would indicate that learning of order is simply not encapsulated by the learning of the items. Experiment 3 is designed to obtain data that can provide for hypothesis testing to assess if order is an additional component of event learning. Thus, if there is strong statistical evidence for the alternative hypothesis, then there would be a good case for the theoretical position that order is a separable and different attribute of memory.

Fifty Tufts undergraduate students volunteered as participants in this experiment in order to partially satisfy a participation requirement in their course (either Introductory Psychology or Introductory Statistics).

The target stimuli were words similar to those used in the previous two experiments. However, in the learning phase of a trial, each of 32 stimuli was presented with one missing letter. The missing letter was a vowel. The participants were instructed to press the letter on the keyboard that corresponded to the missing letter. This procedure is designed to make the presentation of the target items more of a generative task. The generation effect in the memory literature refers to the enhanced learning when the person has to produce or complete the target items; see  Chechile (2018)  for an extensive review of generative encoding procedures and effects. 
                               15 
                             
                            
                               15 
                               
                                  Chechile and Soraci (1999)  studied generative encoding of words for single-trial training and found that  
                                     
                                        
                                           θ 
                                        
                                        
                                           N 
                                           i 
                                        
                                     
                                   was less than  
                                     
                                        . 
                                        04 
                                     
                                  , whereas with standard encoding  
                                     
                                        
                                           θ 
                                        
                                        
                                           N 
                                           i 
                                        
                                     
                                   was greater than  
                                     
                                        . 
                                        19 
                                     
                                  . If order knowledge is linked with item storage, then generative encoding might result in high levels of order learning. But if generative encoding improves item storage but does not improve order storage, then that difference should strengthen the potential for finding statistical support for order being a different attribute of memory. 
                             After the participant typed the correct letter, the intact word was displayed for 2.5 s. before the next item was presented. All the words on a learning list were unrelated. In the test phase of a trial, there were two tests of triplets of words using the testing protocol of the EO model as was described in the previous two experiments. One of the triplets was for the  near  condition, where the words were separated in their serial position by two intervening words, and the other triplet tested was for the  far  condition, where there were 11 intervening words. In addition to these tests for obtaining the data for the EO model, there were two tests using the experimental protocol of the item-storage model. For these tests, a single word from the learning list was presented along with three foil items that were never presented in the learning phase. Following the four tests of order and item knowledge, the trial was completed, and three other trials were presented. After completing the fourth trial for a list, the participant received another list of 32 words for learning in the same fashion. Each participant had four lists. The entire session lasted approximately one hour.

The multinomial frequencies are provided in  Table 7 , and the estimates for both MPT models are provided in  Table 8 . Note that the  near  versus  far  spacing factor does not apply for the item tests; hence the frequencies and estimates for item-storage are listed as the same for the two spacing conditions.
