The discussion in this section is (1) about the overall properties and limitations of the EO model and (2) about some of the theoretical implications from the three experiments.

The EO model is identifiable, and it is a powerful new cognitive measurement tool that provides investigators with a way to explore the memory of order. A major goal for this paper was to establish the method and show that it extracts for each condition, measures for four levels of order knowledge about a triplet of three probed items. The four measures are estimates of probabilities. How are these values to be interpreted? For any given participant on a single trial, there are four states of their knowledge for a specific triplet of events. For this one case, the participant’s knowledge is either (1) complete about the order of the three events, (2) missing only a single order relationship, (3) missing two order relationships, or (4) the null state of no knowledge of order whatsoever. By grouping over participants or grouping over similar tests trials for the same participant, the EO model results in probability values for these four states. The most straightforward way to interpret the probability values is as proportions for the above four knowledge states. In essence the MPT model is a tool to detect and quantify mixture rates. The four states themselves are the logical possibilities given that the testing is for a triplet of items or events. The high construct validity for the EO model is also due to the series of forced-choice tests of the items. Without the follow up testing when there are errors, there would not be sufficient data to enable a decomposition of the mixture over the four possible states of knowledge. The forced-choice method also has the advantage of establishing the guessing rates without requiring free model parameters to deal with guessing. The EO method also has an advantage of being useful regardless of the length of the list of events being probed because any subset of three events can be examined.

A key component of the EO task is the probing for a triplet of events. The choice for using triplets is not arbitrary. The testing of two events was historically a behavioral measure for testing of order ( Fozard, 1970 ). But with only two events it is not possible to differentiate between two states of knowledge because there are two model parameters and only one degree of freedom in the binomial data structure. However, it is shown in this paper that the probing of a triplet with the EO testing protocols results in an identifiable model with four levels of order knowledge. One might wonder if this approach could be expanded to a quartet of items. In principle it is possible to do this, but for a quartet of events there are 24 possible orders. With 24 arrangements of four events, there is a likely problem that participants will lose patience with the testing procedure. The testing would be longer and would be likely to frustrate participants. Also the only benefit for testing a quartet of events would be adding another level of partial-order knowledge, but at the risk of obtaining questionable data. Thus the testing of triplets is arguably an optimal balance of sufficient detail to enable a fine grained analysis of order knowledge while not being too difficult for participants to implement.

Another concern that some might have about the EO procedure is the grouping over a similar set of people or a grouping over a similar set of triplets for the same participant. The grouping is required to have data that results in a meaningful decomposition into the four probability measures for order knowledge. But grouping is not limited to the EO task. Virtually all behavioral dependent measures in experimental psychology involve a similar type of grouping. 
                            16 
                          
                         
                            16 
                            On rare occasions it is possible to have tight stimulus control that seemingly does not depend on grouping. For example, the neuroscience measurements of a single cell in the visual cortex can identify a class of stimuli that elicit a high level of neural firing (e.g.,  Hubel & Wiesel, 1959 ). But even for this case, researchers typically explore a range of different stimuli for the same cell. Moreover the researchers seek to study different cells that have similar behavior and to study other animals in the same brain region. Consequently grouping over a set of tests is still a part of the testing protocols. 
                          Consequently the grouping over a set of test trials is commonplace in the behavioral sciences.

Another limitation of the EO approach is that the testing procedure requires that the participants can understand and execute verbal instructions. Consequently the procedure cannot be used to study order memory for infants, very small children, and patients with severe mental disabilities. Moreover it cannot be used to investigate order memory with nonhuman organisms. 
                            17 
                          
                         
                            17 
                            Model-based measurement with MPT models can be used to study some memory processes in animals. See  Chechile (2018)  for an MPT model for obtaining storage and retrieval measures of memory retention for pigeons. But it is not known at this time how to employ the EO testing protocol without using verbal instructions. 
                          However, the limitation of the EO procedure to humans, who can understand and implement the instructions, is not a major impediment for studying order memory.

The EO testing method can also be cumbersome for assessing some research questions that are specifically about a critical stimulus in a sequence because the testing protocol is based on triplets of stimuli rather than on any one or two stimuli. For example, if a researcher wants to examine the von Restorff effect of better memory of an isolated item within a sequence of items, then the EO testing procedure is possible but awkward. Suppose the distinctively different item is  
                            D 
                          in a sequence of  
                            
                               A 
                               
                               B 
                               
                               C 
                               
                               D 
                               
                               E 
                               
                               F 
                               
                               G 
                            
                         . The order knowledge of triplets that include  
                            D 
                         , such as  
                            
                               B 
                               
                               C 
                               
                               D 
                            
                         ,  
                            
                               C 
                               
                               D 
                               
                               E 
                            
                         , and  
                            
                               D 
                               
                               E 
                               
                               F 
                            
                         , can be contrasted with triplets that do not include the isolated item, such as  
                            
                               A 
                               
                               B 
                               
                               C 
                            
                         . Thus the EO model can be used to study the isolated item, but perhaps at the cost of adding more lists and more participants. But in fairness, a memory question about a single event is typically about the storage and retrieval of that event rather than about the ordinal properties of the stimulus relative to other stimuli.

Finally in the three experiments in this paper, no additional information was collected beyond the categorization of each test trial as a response for one of the four possible multinomial categories. But in subsequent experiments, records can be saved about the specific sequence of alternatives selected. So it is important to explore what can be learned from having the response history for each EO test trial. For the EO task, there are 86 possible response histories. For a cell 1 response (i.e., correct on the 6 AFC test), there is trivially only one history (i.e., ABC). But for a cell 2 response (i.e., correct on the 5 AFC test), there are five possible histories: (1) ACB  
                            → 
                          ABC, (2) BAC  
                            → 
                          ABC, (3) CAB  
                            → 
                          ABC, (4) BCA  
                            → 
                          ABC, and (5) CBA  
                            → 
                          ABC. Some histories can be labeled as  systematic , if it is compatible with a consistent ordering of some of the events. For example a cell 3 response history of ACB  
                            → 
                          BAC  
                            → 
                          ABC is systematic because the participant is consistently ordering event A before event C. But other histories can be labeled as  random  because there is no consistent ordering of the events. An example of a random cell 3 response would be ACB  
                            → 
                          BCA  
                            → 
                          ABC. Only 11 histories are  systematic , and the other 75 histories are  random .

The response history perspective has limitations, but it can also add some additional information. A key limitation of an analysis, based on only the response histories, is that it cannot decompose the case when there is a cell 1 response. The history of ABC does not enable the conclusion that the participant knows all three order relationships because that response is possible for any of the four states of order knowledge as shown in the EO processing tree. Also whenever there is a cell 4 response, then a response history analysis would ascertain that there is no systematic order knowledge. But that inference is the same conclusion that the EO model would make, so nothing is gained in that case from conducting an analysis of the response histories of the cell 4 responses. However, a response history analysis can add some elaborating information when the EO model is employed, and there is either a cell 2 or a cell 3 response. For example, let us consider an analysis of the cell 3 responses for the case from experiment 2, trial 1, near condition, unrelated items, where the observed frequencies are:

                            18 
                          
                         
                            18 
                            The EO model can also make predictions about the number of responses from each order knowledge state for each cell, including cells 1 and 4, with a formula similar to Eq.  (30) .

But with response history information, some constraints can be obtained about the decomposition into those two states. Of the 86 possible response histories, there are 20 histories that correspond to a cell 3 response. Among these 20 histories, there are six systematic histories, which indicate that the participant might have one known order relationship, and there are 14 random histories, which indicate that the participant is guessing. Now suppose that the observed proportion of the 52 cell 3 responses that are systematic were

In a similar fashion, the response history analysis can also help constrain the decomposition of the cell 2 responses into the 2c, 1c, and the null order knowledge states. But as with the response history analysis of the cell 3 responses, the analysis of the cell 2 response histories produces inequalities about the numbers in those three categories. It is not a deterministic decomposition. Thus, beyond these inequalities for the cell 2 and cell 3 responses, the response history analysis adds little to the measurement of order knowledge. Importantly, without the EO model, it would not be possible, based on only the response histories, to measure the probabilities for the four states of order knowledge, which of course is the main purpose of the EO task and model in the first place.

A number of hypotheses and models, which were designed to account for the ability of participants to order stimuli, are problematic in light of the results obtained from the use of the EO model. One such hypothesis is  strength theory  (e.g.,  Hinrichs, 1970 ). From a pure strength theory perspective, the judgment of recency is based on the relative strengths of the items. Stronger events are assumed to be the more recent event. Strength is assumed to be developed based on the initial encoding, but decays as a function of clock time. 
                            19 
                          
                         
                            19 
                            Note that there is no evidence provided for either (1) the idea that strength is an adequate representation of an episodic event, or (2) the idea that the strength decays with time. 
                          If all items are roughly at the same level of initial strength upon encoding, then the stronger trace would be the more recent trace because the amount decayed would be greater for the older items. 
                            20 
                          
                         
                            20 
                            The  Farrell and Lewandowsky (2002)  SOB model uses a distributed memory representation and employs the concept of strength to encode the order of items. Although this model does not assume equal initial strength, it nonetheless evokes the idea that the strength decreases as a function of the ordinal position of a stimulus on a list. Although the model also has other features such as a temporary response suppression of a recalled item, it is essentially a distributed memory form of strength theory for accounting for order. 
                          According to strength theory, the difficulty in recency judgments is reduced with increased item spacing because greater spacing results in more discriminable strength values. So the worse performance should be for adjacent spacing and the best performance should be for far spacing. But we know that order knowledge is superior for the adjacent spacing. Adjacent spacing resulted in better order knowledge than either near or far spacing. So a straightforward prediction of strength theory is not confirmed in the first two experiments. Furthermore, strength theory does not account for the results found from experiment 3. With only a strength value for encoding the information of an event, how can the theory account for the difference between item knowledge versus order knowledge? 
                            21 
                          
                         
                            21 
                            In addition,  Chechile (2018)  provided an extensive critique of strength theory for item memory. 
                          Thus the strength hypothesis has many challenges; it is an implausible account for ordermemory.

The  reconstructive hypothesis  ( Friedman, 1993 ) is another problematic theoretical position that rejects the idea that time or order information is stored in memory. “For example, by remembering that there was snow on a path leading up to the dormitory, a subject could infer that some event must have been during the winter and in the particular year that the subject lived in that dormitory. The essence of a reconstructive model is that aspects of the memory are combined with separate knowledge of time patterns to infer when the event probably occurred.” ( Friedman, 1993 , p. 47). Although we can agree that human reasoning can be used to guide recollection about time and order in autobiographical memory, but this process by itself cannot account for the four states of order knowledge measured with the EO model. Moreover, a reconstructive process is also not a creditable mechanism for accounting for how the probability values of the four knowledge states change as a function of the independent variables examined in the experiments.

A third problematic hypothesis for order learning is the classic  associative chaining model  ( Ebbinghaus, 1885/1913 ). Like the strength model and the reconstructive model, the associative chaining proposal does not require the storage of temporal or ordinal features. Instead the events are linked in an associative chain  
                            
                               A 
                               − 
                               B 
                               − 
                               C 
                               − 
                               ⋯ 
                               
                            
                         . 
                            22 
                          
                         
                            22 
                            It is not argued here that an association is never appropriate in memory theory. For example, in paired-associate learning, order of the stimulus–response pairs ( 
                                  A 
                               – 
                                  B 
                               ) is randomized over each study and test phase of training, so people only can be correct by developing an association between the stimulus and response members of the pair. But this type of association is not a chaining of all the responses as is the claim for serial list learning. For paired-associate learning, the stimulus and response members of a pair are hypothesized to have an associative link between them. Typically it is assumed that the strength of the association for a  
                                  B 
                                response given the  
                                  A 
                                cue is roughly equal to the strength of the  
                                  A 
                                response given the  
                                  B 
                                cue. 
                          But there is an immediate issue about how a bidirectional associative bond can encode order information. There is an implicit assumption with the chaining hypothesis that the item A is the beginning of the chain. So a more appropriate diagram for three events would be  
                            
                               A 
                               ⟶ 
                               B 
                               ⟶ 
                               C 
                            
                         , where the  
                            ⟶ 
                          symbol denotes the flow of time. This change in the representation may seem trivially small, but it is an important theoretical shift. Note this type of linkage between stimuli denotes order and temporal information. It is not simply an association between two events. Presumedly one of the goals of chaining theory was to use only the bidirectional strength linkage to denote time. But a bidirectional link only codes for association; something extra is required to code for the order. Yet even with this change in notation, the chain architecture still is problematic. One problem is the architecture of a linked chain cannot account for the partial order knowledge state where the person knows two of the three order relationships. With the chaining model arrangement, it would be possible either (1) to have complete order knowledge, if both links are intact at the time of test, (2) to have one order relationship known, if a single bond is lost, or (3) to have no order knowledge, if both bonds are destroyed. Yet the experiments in this paper show that it is possible to have the knowledge state of two relationships among the three events. Without an MPT model, an associative-chaining theorist could claim that the data about the apparent knowledge of two of the three is purely the result of guessing (i.e., only one relationship is known plus there is a lucky guess for the other relationship). However, guessing has already been taken into account with the EO model. Consequently, chaining theory must be further modified to account for this problem. A possible modification is to change the connection architecture from a linked chain to a triangle where the stimuli are at the vertices. Moreover the edges of the triangle need to be directional links to encode the relative flow of time between the two vertices. With such a connection arrangement it might be possible to exhibit all four states of order knowledge depending on which links are preserved. Yet even this model would still have difficulties accounting for the spacing findings. A triangular connection arrangement also has to account for the spacing between the stimuli on the list. In traditional association theory, the association is between items that are adjacent in time or space. If remote associations are possible at all in classic association theory, then these would be weaker to the degree of the amount of spacing. But for all three experiments in this paper, far spacing results in better order knowledge than near spacing. So to account for the data, one would need to further modify associative-chaining theory. But this multiply revised model is nearly an unrecognizable version of classical chainingtheory. 
                            23 
                          
                         
                            23 
                            A chaining model is possibly acceptable if people are trained on a serial list to a sufficiently high level where they achieved mastery. However, this learned sequence can undergo memory loss. The EO model has not yet been studied to explore the memory loss after a serial list was learned to mastery. So it is unknown how well a simple chaining model would work when there is forgetting of a once learned list. 
                         
                         
                            24 
                          
                         
                            24 
                            
                               Lewandowsky and Murdock (1989)  provided a model for serial order learning that is based on item-to-item chaining in the context of a distributed memory representation. Bidirectional symmetry is not assumed. Yet for triplet testing in either the near or far spacing conditions, none of the items are cued by the prior item. So it is not clear if this model can account for order judgments on a 6-AFC test for the spaced items. But for adjacent items this concern is not an issue. Yet it still remains a challenge for this model to account for the four states of order knowledge for the items in the triplet.

The failure of the above set of hypotheses, which deny the existence of specific temporal or ordinal information, has an important theoretical consequence. It would seem that either time or order information has to be encoded in memory. That is, memory storage needs to include information about the content of the event along with information about when the event occurred. Thus the experimental results from the EO model confirm the claim by  Underwood (1977, 1983)  that ordinal information is a different attribute of memory. But Underwood was not a memory modeler, so he did not advance a specific theory about how this memory attribute was encoded, learned, accessed, and altered. There are a number theoretical hypotheses about a memory organization that encodes either time or order along with item content information (e.g.,  Brown et al., 2007, 2000; Chechile, 2018; Howard et al., 2015; Lee & Estes, 1981; Ward et al., 2013 ). The study of order memory with the EO task has implications for these theories. Any of the theories would need to have an explanation for the following set of findings: (1) order learning is slower than item learning, (2) order learning is not simply an all-or-none learning of a triplet of events, but rather there are four states of order knowledge, (3) repetition of training improves order knowledge, (4) spacing of events affects the quality of order knowledge encoding, and (5) item relatedness improves order encoding. Some of these findings can fit within the above mentioned models, but it is not clear if any of the models have a ready account for all of these findings.

It is also possible that there might not be a single mechanism by which order knowledge is encoded because the encoding process has many possibilities depending on the mnemonic skills of the person. For example, consider the following four possible mechanisms of encoding for order. One, it might be the case that item-to-item groupings can occur on some occasions, especially for adjacent items. Two, it could also be the case that the person uses a peg-word type mnemonic where each stimulus in the sequence is linked to an internally generated stimulus that carries order information (e.g., a previous rhyme  one bun, two shoe, three tree  
                         
                            … 
                          can be used to learn a sequence as shown by  Bower, 1970 ). Three, it is possible that a semantic associate of the current item is activated, and if this associate was a prior item, then the relative order between these two items could be easily stored. This mechanism would be more likely for semantically related items. Four, it might also be the case that there are temporal “islands” encoded along with the content information of the items (i.e., the items are encoded with a coarse time tag). Thus when asked about items, which come from different temporal regions, then it might be possible to infer the relative order from the time tags. This mechanism would be especially effective for items that are spaced far apart. Although there is a scientific preference to look for a single mechanism for order encoding, it might be difficult to account for all the effects found with the EO model with a single mechanism.

In conclusion it seems appropriate to observe that for far too long the research on event memory has focused on the informational content of the events. But our understanding of episodic memory is incomplete without delineating how people know the relative order of events. Moreover, the experimental studies of order memory have been handicapped by a lack of a correction for guessing, and the lack of a measurement method to see the degrees of order knowledge. The EO task and model are designed to solve those measurement shortcomings. The experiments in this paper show that order knowledge is improved with the repetition of training, but it still is incomplete after four training trials. It will be important to track, with the EO task and model, how order knowledge builds toward mastery as a function of various learning techniques and levels of practice. Furthermore, other studies will be needed to explore the forgetting of previously learned order knowledge. Ultimately, it will be important to explore how the new information acquired about order learning and forgetting fits into an overall theoretical model or a simulation of episodic memory.

The following R code implements 50,000 random samples for the parameters of the EO model. Importantly, the user must change the values of n1, n2, n3, and n4 to the appropriate frequencies that the user wishes to analyze.

The program runs in base R in seconds. After running the program, there are R vectors for each of the four parameters of the EO model. The user can then find point estimates via additional standard R commands. For quantiles of the posterior distribution, the user can use the R quantile commands. The value of the coherence probability is the probcoh value that is resident in the R workspace.

The software for doing the item-storage analysis for the data about item knowledge that was used in experiment 3 is given below. The software is based on the PPM estimation procedure.

In Section  3.2  the MLE are provided for the case when there are no outcome categories with a frequency of zero. For the case when all the frequencies are zero, there is not a unique maximum of the likelihood function because all combinations of the EO parameters have the same likelihood (i.e., it is a flat likelihood in the three dimensional simplex). There are 14 other special cases where there is at least one category with a frequency equal to zero. These cases are described below along with the resulting MLE solution.
